style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, 4.9% <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niemann_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niemann-9">[9]</a></sup><br/><span class="nowrap">97% <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap">2.7±0.1% <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap">0.1–0.2% <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155-10">[10]</a></sup></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Titan</b> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">moon of Saturn</a>. It is the only <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moon</a> known to have a dense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, and the only object in space, other than <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, where clear evidence of stable bodies of surface liquid have been found.
</p><p>Titan is the sixth <a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitationally_rounded_objects_of_the_Solar_System" title="List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System">gravitationally rounded</a> moon from <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. Frequently described as a planet-like moon, Titan is 50% larger than Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and it is 80% more massive. It is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_by_diameter" title="List of natural satellites by diameter">second-largest moon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, after Jupiter's moon <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and is larger than the smallest planet, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, but only 40% as massive. Discovered in 1655 by the Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>, Titan was the first known moon of Saturn, and the sixth known planetary satellite (after Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and the four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons of Jupiter</a>). Titan orbits Saturn at 20 Saturn radii. From Titan's surface, Saturn subtends an arc of 5.09 degrees and would appear 11.4 times larger in the sky than the Moon from Earth.
</p><p>Titan is primarily composed of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a> and rocky material. Much as with <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">Space Age</a>, the dense opaque atmosphere prevented understanding of Titan's surface until new information from the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> mission in 2004, including the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrocarbon" title="Liquid hydrocarbon">liquid hydrocarbon</a> lakes in Titan's polar regions. The geologically young surface is generally smooth, with few <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a>, although mountains and several possible <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryovolcanoes</a> have been found.
</p><p>The atmosphere of Titan is largely <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>; minor components lead to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> clouds and nitrogen-rich organic smog. The climate—including wind and rain—creates surface features similar to those of Earth, such as dunes, rivers, lakes, seas (probably of liquid methane and ethane), and deltas, and is dominated by seasonal weather patterns as on Earth. With its liquids (both surface and subsurface) and robust nitrogen atmosphere, Titan's methane cycle is analogous to Earth's <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, at the much lower temperature of about 94 K (−179.2 °C; −290.5 °F).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2204" data-file-width="1682" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg/170px-Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg/255px-Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg/340px-Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christiaan_Huygens-painting.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> discovered Titan in 1655.</div></div></div>
<p>Titan was discovered on March 25, 1655 by the Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Huygens was inspired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>'s discovery of Jupiter's four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">largest moons</a> in 1610 and his improvements in <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> technology. Christiaan, with the help of his older brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens,_Jr." title="Constantijn Huygens, Jr.">Constantijn Huygens, Jr.</a>, began building telescopes around 1650 and discovered the first observed moon orbiting Saturn with one of the telescopes they built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It was the sixth moon ever discovered, after Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huygens named his discovery <i>Saturni Luna</i> (or <i>Luna Saturni</i>, Latin for "Saturn's moon"), publishing in the 1655 tract <i>De Saturni Luna Observatio Nova</i> (<i>A New Observation of Saturn's Moon</i>). After <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a> published his discoveries of four more moons of Saturn between 1673 and 1686, astronomers fell into the habit of referring to these and Titan as Saturn I through V (with Titan then in fourth position). Other early epithets for Titan include "Saturn's ordinary satellite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Titan is officially numbered <b>Saturn VI</b> because after the 1789 discoveries the numbering scheme was frozen to avoid causing any more confusion (Titan having borne the numbers II and IV as well as VI). Numerous small moons have been discovered closer to Saturn since then.
</p><p>The name <i>Titan</i>, and the names of all seven satellites of Saturn then known, came from <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> (son of <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>, discoverer of two other Saturnian moons, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>), in his 1847 publication <i>Results of Astronomical Observations Made during the Years 1834, 5, 6, 7, 8, at the Cape of Good Hope</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He suggested the names of the mythological <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A4%CE%B9%CF%84%CE%AC%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:Τιτάν">Τῑτᾶνες</a></span>), brothers and sisters of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>, the Greek Saturn. In Greek mythology, the Titans were a race of powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deities" title="Deities">deities</a>, descendants of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a>, that ruled during the legendary <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan%27s_orbit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="984" data-file-width="955" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Titan%27s_orbit.svg/220px-Titan%27s_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Titan%27s_orbit.svg/330px-Titan%27s_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Titan%27s_orbit.svg/440px-Titan%27s_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan%27s_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titan's orbit (highlighted in red) among the other large inner moons of Saturn. The moons outside its orbit are (from the outside to the inside) Iapetus and Hyperion; those inside are Rhea, Dione, Tethys, Enceladus, and Mimas.</div></div></div>
<p>Titan orbits Saturn once every 15 days and 22 hours. Like the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and many of the satellites of the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_period" title="Rotational period">rotational period</a> (its day) is identical to its orbital period; Titan is <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a> in synchronous rotation with Saturn, and permanently shows one face to the planet, so Titan's "day" is equal to its orbit period. Because of this, there is a sub-Saturnian point on its surface, from which the planet would always appear to hang directly overhead. Longitudes on Titan are measured westward, starting from the meridian passing through this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Its orbital eccentricity is 0.0288, and the orbital plane is inclined 0.348 degrees relative to the Saturnian equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horizons_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-horizons-2">[2]</a></sup> Viewed from Earth, Titan reaches an angular distance of about 20 Saturn radii (just over 1,200,000 kilometers (750,000 mi)) from Saturn and subtends a disk 0.8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a> in diameter.
</p><p>The small, irregularly shaped satellite <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">Hyperion</a> is locked in a 3:4 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Titan. A "slow and smooth" evolution of the resonance—in which Hyperion migrated from a chaotic orbit—is considered unlikely, based on models. Hyperion probably formed in a stable orbital island, whereas the massive Titan absorbed or ejected bodies that made close approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulk_characteristics">Bulk characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bulk characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/300px-Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/450px-Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/600px-Titan%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Size comparison: Titan (<i>lower left</i>) with the Moon and Earth (<i>top and right</i>)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan_poster.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="2100" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Titan_poster.svg/300px-Titan_poster.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Titan_poster.svg/450px-Titan_poster.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Titan_poster.svg/600px-Titan_poster.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A model of Titan's internal structure showing <a href="/wiki/Ice#Phases" title="Ice">ice-six</a> layer</div></div></div></div>
<p>Titan is 5,149.46 kilometers (3,199.73 mi) in diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zebker_2018_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zebker_2018-3">[3]</a></sup> 1.06 times that of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, 1.48 that of the Moon, and 0.40 that of Earth. Before the arrival of <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> in 1980, Titan was thought to be slightly larger than <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> (diameter 5,262 kilometers (3,270 mi)) and thus the largest moon in the Solar System; this was an overestimation caused by Titan's dense, opaque atmosphere, which extends many kilometres above its surface and increases its apparent diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets-20">[20]</a></sup> Titan's diameter and mass (and thus its density) are similar to those of the Jovian moons Ganymede and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LunineAstro_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-LunineAstro-21">[21]</a></sup> Based on its bulk density of 1.88 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, Titan's composition is half water ice and half rocky material. Though similar in composition to <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, it is denser due to <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_compression" title="Gravitational compression">gravitational compression</a>. It has a mass 1/4226 that of Saturn, making it the largest moon of the gas giants relative to the mass of its primary. It is second in terms of relative diameter of moons to a gas giant; Titan being 1/22.609 of Saturn's diameter, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> is larger in diameter relative to <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> at 1/18.092.
</p><p>Titan is likely differentiated into several layers with a 3,400-kilometer (2,100 mi) rocky center surrounded by several layers composed of different crystalline forms of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobie_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobie-22">[22]</a></sup> Its interior may still be hot enough for a liquid layer consisting of a "<a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a>" composed of water and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Ice_Ih" title="Ice Ih">ice I<sub>h</sub></a> crust and deeper ice layers made of high-pressure forms of ice. The presence of ammonia allows water to remain liquid even at a temperature as low as 176 K (−97 °C) (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic" title="Eutectic">eutectic</a> mixture with water).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longstaff_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-longstaff-23">[23]</a></sup>  The <i>Cassini</i> probe discovered the evidence for the layered structure in the form of natural <a href="/wiki/Extremely_low_frequency" title="Extremely low frequency">extremely-low-frequency</a> radio waves in Titan's atmosphere. Titan's surface is thought to be a poor reflector of extremely-low-frequency radio waves, so they may instead be reflecting off the liquid–ice boundary of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">subsurface ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Titan_ELF_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Titan_ELF-24">[24]</a></sup> Surface features were observed by the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft to systematically shift by up to 30 kilometers (19 mi) between October 2005 and May 2007, which suggests that the crust is decoupled from the interior, and provides additional evidence for an interior liquid layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS2008_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NS2008-25">[25]</a></sup>  Further supporting evidence for a liquid layer and ice shell decoupled from the solid core comes from the way the gravity field varies as Titan orbits Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iess_et_al._2012_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iess_et_al._2012-26">[26]</a></sup> Comparison of the gravity field with the RADAR-based topography observations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> also suggests that the ice shell may be substantially rigid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemingway_et_al._2013_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hemingway_et_al._2013-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl.nasa.gov_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl.nasa.gov-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The moons of Jupiter and Saturn are thought to have formed through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">co-accretion</a>, a similar process to that believed to have formed the planets in the Solar System. As the young gas giants formed, they were surrounded by discs of material that gradually coalesced into moons. Whereas Jupiter possesses four large satellites in highly regular, planet-like orbits, Titan overwhelmingly dominates Saturn's system and possesses a high orbital eccentricity not immediately explained by co-accretion alone. A proposed model for the formation of Titan is that Saturn's system began with a group of moons similar to Jupiter's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_satellites" title="Galilean satellites">Galilean satellites</a>, but that they were disrupted by a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact" title="Giant impact">giant impacts</a>, which would go on to form Titan. Saturn's mid-sized moons, such as <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, were formed from the debris of these collisions. Such a violent beginning would also explain Titan's orbital eccentricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, analysis of Titan's atmospheric nitrogen suggested that it has possibly been sourced from material similar to that found in the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> and not from sources present during co-accretion of materials around Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201450623_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-201450623-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">Atmosphere of Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="972" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/170px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/255px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/340px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>True-color image of layers of haze in Titan's atmosphere</div></div></div>
<p>Titan is the only known moon with a significant <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NasaNews.moons_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NasaNews.moons-32">[32]</a></sup> and its atmosphere is the only nitrogen-rich dense atmosphere in the Solar System aside from Earth's. Observations of it made in 2004 by <i>Cassini</i> suggest that Titan is a "super rotator", like Venus, with an atmosphere that rotates much faster than its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Observations from the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i></a> space probes have shown that Titan's atmosphere is denser than Earth's, with a surface pressure about 1.45 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>. It is also about 1.19 times as massive as Earth's overall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008130_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008130-34">[34]</a></sup> or about 7.3 times more massive on a per surface area basis. <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">Opaque</a> haze layers block most visible light from the Sun and other sources and obscures Titan's surface features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zubrin_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zubrin-35">[35]</a></sup> Titan's lower gravity means that its atmosphere is far more extended than Earth's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The atmosphere of Titan is <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque</a> at many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavelengths" title="Wavelengths">wavelengths</a> and as a result, a complete reflectance spectrum of the surface is impossible to acquire from orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It was not until the arrival of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> spacecraft in 2004 that the first direct images of Titan's surface were obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="774" data-file-width="1203" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg/220px-PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg/330px-PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg/440px-PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18431-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPoleVortex-Cloud-20121129.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titan's South Pole Vortex—a swirling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_Cyanide" title="Hydrogen Cyanide">HCN</a> gas cloud (November 29, 2012).</div></div></div>
<p>Titan's atmospheric composition is nitrogen (97%), methane (2.7±0.1%), hydrogen (0.1–0.2%) with trace amounts of other gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155-10">[10]</a></sup> There are trace amounts of other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diacetylene" title="Diacetylene">diacetylene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylacetylene" title="Methylacetylene">methylacetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, and of other gases, such as <a href="/wiki/Cyanoacetylene" title="Cyanoacetylene">cyanoacetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niemann_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Niemann-9">[9]</a></sup> The hydrocarbons are thought to form in Titan's upper atmosphere in reactions resulting from the breakup of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> by the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light, producing a thick orange smog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waite_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-waite-39">[39]</a></sup> Titan spends 95% of its time within Saturn's magnetosphere, which may help shield it from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Energy from the Sun should have converted all traces of methane in Titan's atmosphere into more complex hydrocarbons within 50 million years—a short time compared to the age of the Solar System. This suggests that methane must be replenished by a reservoir on or within Titan itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The ultimate origin of the methane in its atmosphere may be its interior, released via eruptions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanoes" title="Cryovolcanoes">cryovolcanoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.sci.76_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.sci.76-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hydrocarbon_lakes_on_Titan_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hydrocarbon_lakes_on_Titan-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4600" data-file-width="4600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/220px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/330px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/440px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sunset studies on Titan by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_(spacecraft)" title="Cassini (spacecraft)"><i>Cassini</i></a> help to better understand <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> (artist's concept).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="846" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg/220px-Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg/330px-Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg/440px-Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan-SaturnMoon-Maps-TraceGases-20141022.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">Trace</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> gases in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">Titan's atmosphere</a>—<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_isocyanide" title="Hydrogen isocyanide">HNC</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Cyanoacetylene" title="Cyanoacetylene">HC<sub>3</sub>N</a> (right).</div></div></div>
<p>On April 3, 2013, NASA reported that complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_chemicals" title="Organic chemicals">organic chemicals</a>, collectively called <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>, likely arise on Titan, based on studies simulating the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)#Atmosphere" title="Titan (moon)">atmosphere</a> of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysOrg-20130403_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysOrg-20130403-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 6, 2013, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Andaluc%C3%ADa" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Andalucía">IAA-CSIC</a> reported the detection of <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> in the upper atmosphere of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAA-20130606_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAA-20130606-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 30, 2013, <a href="/wiki/Propene" title="Propene">propene</a> was detected in the atmosphere of Titan by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft, using its composite infrared spectrometer (CIRS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130930_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130930-49">[49]</a></sup> This is the first time propene has been found on any moon or planet other than Earth and is the first chemical found by the CIRS. The detection of propene fills a mysterious gap in observations that date back to NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> spacecraft's first close <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">planetary flyby</a> of Titan in 1980, during which it was discovered that many of the gases that make up Titan's brown haze were hydrocarbons, theoretically formed via the recombination of radicals created by the Sun's ultraviolet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waite_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-waite-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 24, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> was found in polar clouds on Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141024-PD_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141024-PD-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141024-EZ_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141024-EZ-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:608px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg" title="Polar clouds, made of methane, on Titan (left) compared with polar clouds on Earth (right), which are made of water or water ice."><img alt="" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="2000" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg/600px-Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg/900px-Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg/1200px-Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg" title="File:Titan-Earth-PolarClouds-20141024.jpg"> </a></div>Polar clouds, made of methane, on Titan (left) compared with <a href="/wiki/Polar_stratospheric_cloud" title="Polar stratospheric cloud">polar clouds</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> (right), which are made of water or water ice.</div>
</div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Titan" title="Climate of Titan">Climate of Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="515" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png/220px-Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png/330px-Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png/440px-Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vortex_on_saturn%27s_moon_titan.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a> over Titan's south pole</div></div></div>
<p>Titan's surface temperature is about 94 K (−179.2 °C). At this temperature, water ice has an extremely low <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a>, so the little <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> present appears limited to the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottiniNixon2012_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-CottiniNixon2012-52">[52]</a></sup> Titan receives about 1% as much sunlight as Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Before sunlight reaches the surface, about 90% has been absorbed by the thick atmosphere, leaving only 0.1% of the amount of light Earth receives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atmospheric methane creates a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> on Titan's surface, without which Titan would be far colder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oil_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-oil-55">[55]</a></sup>  Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">haze</a> in Titan's atmosphere contributes to an <a href="/wiki/Anti-greenhouse_effect" title="Anti-greenhouse effect">anti-greenhouse effect</a> by reflecting sunlight back into space, cancelling a portion of the greenhouse effect and making its surface significantly colder than its upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="727" data-file-width="727" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif/170px-PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif/255px-PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif/340px-PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18420-Titan-MethaneClouds-20140722.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Methane clouds (animated; July 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140812_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140812-57">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Titan's clouds, probably composed of methane, ethane or other simple organics, are scattered and variable, punctuating the overall haze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets-20">[20]</a></sup>  The findings of the <i>Huygens</i> probe indicate that Titan's atmosphere periodically rains liquid methane and other organic compounds onto its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary-Arizona_Icebox_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-Arizona_Icebox-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clouds typically cover 1% of Titan's disk, though outburst events have been observed in which the cloud cover rapidly expands to as much as 8%. One hypothesis asserts that the southern clouds are formed when heightened <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">levels of sunlight</a> during the southern summer generate uplift in the atmosphere, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>. This explanation is complicated by the fact that cloud formation has been observed not only after the southern summer solstice but also during mid-spring. Increased methane humidity at the south pole possibly contributes to the rapid increases in cloud size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It was summer in Titan's southern hemisphere until 2010, when Saturn's orbit, which governs Titan's motion, moved Titan's northern hemisphere into the sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Titan_wind_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Titan_wind-60">[60]</a></sup> When the seasons switch, it is expected that ethane will begin to condense over the south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">Surface features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Surface features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Titan" title="List of geological features on Titan">List of geological features on Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4950" data-file-width="4800" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg/220px-PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg/330px-PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg/440px-PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21923-Titan-SaturnMoon-InfraredViews-20180718.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center> Titan – infrared views (2004 – 2017)</center></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4442" data-file-width="8546" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg/220px-PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg/330px-PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg/440px-PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20713-Titan-SaturnMoon-LabeledFeaturesIAU-June2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Global map of Titan – with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAU" title="IAU">IAU</a> labels (August 2016).</center></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:258px;max-width:258px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3208" data-file-width="3208" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg/125px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg/188px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg/250px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-NorthPole-20140407.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><center>North pole</center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3108" data-file-width="3108" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg/125px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg/188px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg/250px-PIA19657-SaturnMoon-Titan-SouthPole-20140407.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><center>South pole</center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><center>Titan (2014)</center></div></div></div>
<p>The surface of Titan has been described as "complex, fluid-processed, [and] geologically young".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Titan has been around since the Solar System's formation, but its surface is much younger, between 100 million and 1 billion years old. Geological processes may have reshaped Titan's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puzzling_geologic_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-puzzling_geologic-63">[63]</a></sup> Titan's atmosphere is twice as thick as Earth's, making it difficult for astronomical instruments to image its surface in the visible light spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft used infrared instruments, radar altimetry and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">synthetic aperture radar</a> (SAR) imaging to map portions of Titan during its close fly-bys. The first images revealed a diverse geology, with both rough and smooth areas. There are features that may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">volcanic</a> in origin, disgorging water mixed with ammonia onto the surface. There is also evidence that Titan's ice shell may be substantially rigid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemingway_et_al._2013_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hemingway_et_al._2013-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl.nasa.gov_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl.nasa.gov-29">[29]</a></sup> which would suggest little geologic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_and_Pappalardo_2011_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_and_Pappalardo_2011-65">[65]</a></sup>
There are also streaky features, some of them hundreds of kilometers in length, that appear to be caused by windblown particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Examination has also shown the surface to be relatively smooth; the few objects that seem to be impact craters appeared to have been filled in, perhaps by raining hydrocarbons or volcanoes. Radar altimetry suggests height variation is low, typically no more than 150 meters. Occasional elevation changes of 500 meters have been discovered and Titan has mountains that sometimes reach several hundred meters to more than 1 kilometer in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titan's surface is marked by broad regions of bright and dark terrain. These include <a href="/wiki/Xanadu_(Titan)" title="Xanadu (Titan)">Xanadu</a>, a large, <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflective</a> equatorial area about the size of Australia. It was first identified in <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> images from the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in 1994, and later viewed by the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft. The convoluted region is filled with hills and cut by valleys and chasms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It is criss-crossed in places by dark lineaments—sinuous topographical features resembling ridges or crevices. These may represent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> activity, which would indicate that Xanadu is geologically young. Alternatively, the lineaments may be liquid-formed channels, suggesting old terrain that has been cut through by stream systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> There are dark areas of similar size elsewhere on Titan, observed from the ground and by <i>Cassini</i>; at least one of these, <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a>, Titan's second-largest sea, is almost a pure methane sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methane_sea_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-methane_sea-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Le_Gall_2016_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Gall_2016-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lakes">Lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">Lakes of Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1265" data-file-width="1691" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg/400px-PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg/600px-PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg/800px-PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22481-SaturnMoon-Titan-Lakes-20170911.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Titan lakes (September 11, 2017)</center></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="2348" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/220px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/330px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/440px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color <i>Cassini</i> radar mosaic of Titan's north polar region. Blue coloring indicates low radar reflectivity, caused by hydrocarbon seas, lakes and tributary networks filled with liquid ethane, methane and dissolved <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoustenisTaylor2008154–155-10">[10]</a></sup> About half of the large body at lower left, <a href="/wiki/Kraken_Mare" title="Kraken Mare">Kraken Mare</a>, is shown. <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a> is at lower right.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg/220px-HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg/330px-HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg/440px-HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HRICoastLineMoasic_H.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mosaic of three <i>Huygens</i> images of channel system on Titan</div></div></div>
<p>The possibility of hydrocarbon seas on Titan was first suggested based on <i>Voyager 1</i> and <i>2</i> data that showed Titan to have a thick atmosphere of approximately the correct temperature and composition to support them, but direct evidence was not obtained until 1995 when data from Hubble and other observations suggested the existence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_methane" title="Liquid methane">liquid methane</a> on Titan, either in disconnected pockets or on the scale of satellite-wide oceans, similar to water on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Cassini</i> mission confirmed the former hypothesis. When the probe arrived in the Saturnian system in 2004, it was hoped that hydrocarbon lakes or oceans would be detected from the sunlight reflected off their surface, but no <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specular reflections</a> were initially observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Near Titan's south pole, an enigmatic dark feature named <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Lacus" title="Ontario Lacus">Ontario Lacus</a> was identified<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> (and later confirmed to be a lake).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.20080730_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.20080730-76">[76]</a></sup> A possible shoreline was also identified near the pole via radar imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Following a flyby on July 22, 2006, in which the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft's radar imaged the northern latitudes (that were then in winter), several large, smooth (and thus dark to radar) patches were seen dotting the surface near the pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIA08630_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-PIA08630-78">[78]</a></sup> Based on the observations, scientists announced "definitive evidence of lakes filled with methane on Saturn's moon Titan" in January 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureDefinitive_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatureDefinitive-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The <i>Cassini–Huygens</i> team concluded that the imaged features are almost certainly the long-sought hydrocarbon lakes, the first stable bodies of surface liquid found outside Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureDefinitive_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-NatureDefinitive-79">[79]</a></sup> Some appear to have channels associated with liquid and lie in topographical depressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureDefinitive_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-NatureDefinitive-79">[79]</a></sup> The liquid erosion features appear to be a very recent occurrence: channels in some regions have created surprisingly little erosion, suggesting erosion on Titan is extremely slow, or some other recent phenomena may have wiped out older riverbeds and landforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puzzling_geologic_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-puzzling_geologic-63">[63]</a></sup> Overall, the <i>Cassini</i> radar observations have shown that lakes cover only a small percentage of the surface, making Titan much drier than Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Most of the lakes are concentrated near the poles (where the relative lack of sunlight prevents evaporation), but several long-standing hydrocarbon lakes in the equatorial desert regions have also been discovered, including one near the <i>Huygens</i> landing site in the Shangri-La region, which is about half the size of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Salt_Lake" title="Great Salt Lake">Great Salt Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, USA. The equatorial lakes are probably "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oases" title="Oases">oases</a>", i.e. the likely supplier is underground <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:404px;max-width:404px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg" title="File:PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="2000" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg/400px-PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg/600px-PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg/800px-PIA18430-SaturnMoon-Titan-EvolvingFeature-20140821.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg" title="File:PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1496" data-file-width="2000" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg/400px-PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg/600px-PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg/800px-PIA20021-SaturnMoonTitan-MagicIsland-20160302.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center;background-color:">Evolving feature in <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a></div></div></div>
<p>In June 2008, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_and_Neutral_Mass_Spectrometer" title="Ion and Neutral Mass Spectrometer">Visual and Infrared Mapping Spectrometer</a> on <i>Cassini</i> confirmed the presence of liquid ethane beyond doubt in Ontario Lacus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> On December 21, 2008, <i>Cassini</i> passed directly over Ontario Lacus and observed specular reflection in radar. The strength of the reflection saturated the probe's receiver, indicating that the lake level did not vary by more than 3 mm (implying either that surface winds were minimal, or the lake's hydrocarbon fluid is viscous).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wye_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wye-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Specular_Spectacular_(PIA18432).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2002" data-file-width="2002" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg/220px-Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg/330px-Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg/440px-Specular_Spectacular_%28PIA18432%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Specular_Spectacular_(PIA18432).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Near-infrared radiation from the Sun reflecting off Titan's hydrocarbon seas</div></div></div>
<p>On July 8, 2009, <i>Cassini's</i> VIMS observed a specular reflection indicative of a smooth, mirror-like surface, off what today is called <a href="/wiki/Jingpo_Lacus" title="Jingpo Lacus">Jingpo Lacus</a>, a lake in the north polar region shortly after the area emerged from 15 years of winter darkness. Specular reflections are indicative of a smooth, mirror-like surface, so the observation corroborated the inference of the presence of a large liquid body drawn from radar imaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-press_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-press-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIMS_specular_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-VIMS_specular-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early radar measurements made in July 2009 and January 2010 indicated that Ontario Lacus was extremely shallow, with an average depth of 0.4–3 m, and a maximum depth of 3 to 7 m (9.8 to 23.0 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20101217_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdc20101217-88">[88]</a></sup> In contrast, the northern hemisphere's <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a> was initially mapped to depths exceeding 8 m, the maximum discernable by the radar instrument and the analysis techniques of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20101217_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-sdc20101217-88">[88]</a></sup>
Later science analysis, released in 2014, more fully mapped the depths of Titan's three methane seas and showed depths of more than 200 meters (660 ft). <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a> averages from 20 to 40 m (66 to 131 ft) in depth, while other parts of <i>Ligeia</i> did not register any radar reflection at all, indicating a depth of more than 200 m (660 ft). While only the second largest of Titan's methane seas, <i>Ligeia</i> "contains enough liquid methane to fill three <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigans</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20141117_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20141117-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>During a flyby on 26 September 2012, <i>Cassini'</i>s radar detected in Titan's northern polar region what is likely a river with a length of more than 400 kilometers. It has been compared with the much larger <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> river on Earth. This feature is connected to <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.20080730_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.20080730-76">[76]</a></sup> Later, a paper ("Liquid-filled Canyons on Titan"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>) published on Geophysical Research Letters on 9 August 2016 reported about the May 2013 Cassini RADAR altimeter observation of Vid Flumina channels, defined as a drainage network connected to Titan's second largest hydrocarbon sea, Ligeia Mare. Analysis of the received altimeter echoes showed that the channels are located in deep (up to ~570 m), steep-sided, canyons and have strong specular surface reflections that indicate they are currently liquid filled. Elevations of the liquid in these channels are at the same level as Ligeia Mare to within a vertical precision of about 0.7 m, consistent with the interpretation of drowned river valleys. Specular reflections are also observed in lower order tributaries elevated above the level of Ligeia Mare, consistent with drainage feeding into the main channel system. This is likely the first direct evidence of the presence of liquid channels on Titan and the first observation of hundred-meter deep canyons on Titan. Vid Flumina canyons are thus drowned by the sea but there are a few isolated observations to attest to the presence of surface liquids standing at higher elevations.
</p><p>During six flybys of Titan from 2006 to 2011, <i>Cassini</i> gathered radiometric tracking and optical navigation data from which investigators could roughly infer Titan's changing shape. The density of Titan is consistent with a body that is about 60% rock and 40% water. The team's analyses suggest that Titan's surface can rise and fall by up to 10 metres during each orbit. That degree of warping suggests that Titan's interior is relatively deformable, and that the most likely model of Titan is one in which an icy shell dozens of kilometres thick floats atop a global ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tides_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tides-91">[91]</a></sup> The team's findings, together with the results of previous studies, hint that Titan's ocean may lie no more than 100 kilometers (62 mi) below its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tides_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tides-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Titan_ocean_of_water_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Titan_ocean_of_water-92">[92]</a></sup> On July 2, 2014, NASA reported the ocean inside Titan may be as salty as the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140702_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140702-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICARUS-2014_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICARUS-2014-94">[94]</a></sup> On September 3, 2014, NASA reported studies suggesting <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> rainfall on Titan may interact with a layer of icy materials underground, called an "alkanofer", to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> that may eventually feed into rivers and lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140903_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140903-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, Cassini found the first evidence of fluid-filled channels on Titan, in a series of deep, steep-sided canyons flowing into <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a>. This network of canyons, dubbed Vid Flumina, range in depth from 240 to 570 m and have sides as steep as 40°. They are believed to have formed either by crustal uplifting, like Earth's <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a>, or a lowering of sea level, or perhaps a combination of the two. The depth of erosion suggests that liquid flows in this part of Titan are long-term features that persist for thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
