<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_craters">Impact craters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Impact craters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titancrater.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="725" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Titancrater.jpg/220px-Titancrater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Titancrater.jpg/330px-Titancrater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Titancrater.jpg/440px-Titancrater.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titancrater.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radar image of a 139 km-diameter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood2009_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2009-97">[97]</a></sup> impact crater on Titan's surface, showing a smooth floor, rugged rim, and possibly a <a href="/wiki/Complex_crater" title="Complex crater">central peak</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Radar, SAR and imaging data from <i>Cassini</i> have revealed few impact craters on Titan's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puzzling_geologic_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-puzzling_geologic-63">[63]</a></sup> These impacts appear to be relatively young, compared to Titan's age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puzzling_geologic_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-puzzling_geologic-63">[63]</a></sup> The few impact craters discovered include a 440-kilometer-wide (270 mi) two-ring impact basin named Menrva seen by <i>Cassini's</i> ISS as a bright-dark concentric pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> A smaller, 60-kilometer-wide (37 mi), flat-floored crater named Sinlap<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and a 30 km (19 mi) crater with a central peak and dark floor named Ksa have also been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Radar and <i>Cassini</i> imaging have also revealed "crateriforms", circular features on the surface of Titan that may be impact related, but lack certain features that would make identification certain. For example, a 90-kilometer-wide (56 mi) ring of bright, rough material known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guabonito_(Titan)" title="Guabonito (Titan)">Guabonito</a> has been observed by <i>Cassini</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guabonito_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guabonito-101">[101]</a></sup> This feature is thought to be an impact crater filled in by dark, windblown sediment. Several other similar features have been observed in the dark Shangri-la and Aaru regions. Radar observed several circular features that may be craters in the bright region Xanadu during <i>Cassini's</i> April 30, 2006 flyby of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xcraters_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xcraters-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1285" data-file-width="2980" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg/220px-PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg/330px-PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg/440px-PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19052-SaturnMoon-Titan-LigeiaMare-SAR%26DespeckledViews-20150212.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">SAR</a> and clearer despeckled views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucas2014_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucas2014-103">[103]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Many of Titan's craters or probable craters display evidence of extensive erosion, and all show some indication of modification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood2009_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2009-97">[97]</a></sup> Most large craters have breached or incomplete rims, despite the fact that some craters on Titan have relatively more massive rims than those anywhere else in the Solar System. There is little evidence of formation of <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest_(planetary_astronomy)" title="Palimpsest (planetary astronomy)">palimpsests</a> through viscoelastic crustal relaxation, unlike on other large icy moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood2009_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2009-97">[97]</a></sup> Most craters lack central peaks and have smooth floors, possibly due to impact-generation or later eruption of <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryovolcanic lava</a>. Infill from various geological processes is one reason for Titan's relative deficiency of craters; atmospheric shielding also plays a role. It is estimated that Titan's atmosphere reduces the number of craters on its surface by a factor of two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The limited high-resolution radar coverage of Titan obtained through 2007 (22%) suggested the existence of nonuniformities in its crater distribution. <a href="/wiki/Xanadu_(Titan)" title="Xanadu (Titan)">Xanadu</a> has 2–9 times more craters than elsewhere. The leading hemisphere has a 30% higher density than the trailing hemisphere. There are lower crater densities in areas of equatorial dunes and in the north polar region (where hydrocarbon lakes and seas are most common).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood2009_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2009-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pre-<i>Cassini</i> models of impact trajectories and angles suggest that where the impactor strikes the water ice crust, a small amount of ejecta remains as liquid water within the crater. It may persist as liquid for centuries or longer, sufficient for "the synthesis of simple precursor molecules to the origin of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cryovolcanism_and_mountains">Cryovolcanism and mountains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cryovolcanism and mountains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">Cryovolcano</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tortola_Facula_in_infrared.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Tortola_Facula_in_infrared.jpg/220px-Tortola_Facula_in_infrared.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Tortola_Facula_in_infrared.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tortola_Facula_in_infrared.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Near-infrared image of Tortola Facula, thought to be a possible cryovolcano</div></div></div>
<p>Scientists have long speculated that conditions on Titan resemble those of early Earth, though at a much lower temperature. The detection of argon-40 in the atmosphere in 2004 indicated that volcanoes had spawned plumes of "lava" composed of water and ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Global maps of the lake distribution on Titan's surface revealed that there is not enough surface methane to account for its continued presence in its atmosphere, and thus that a significant portion must be added through volcanic processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-rain-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Still, there is a paucity of surface features that can be unambiguously interpreted as cryovolcanoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2008_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2008-108">[108]</a></sup> One of the first of such features revealed by <i>Cassini</i> radar observations in 2004, called <a href="/wiki/Ganesa_Macula" title="Ganesa Macula">Ganesa Macula</a>, resembles the geographic features called "<a href="/wiki/Pancake_dome" title="Pancake dome">pancake domes</a>" found on Venus, and was thus initially thought to be cryovolcanic in origin, until Kirk et al. refuted this hypothesis at the <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a> annual meeting in December 2008. The feature was found to be not a dome at all, but appeared to result from accidental combination of light and dark patches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In 2004 <i>Cassini</i> also detected an unusually bright feature (called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tortola_Facula&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tortola Facula (page does not exist)">Tortola Facula</a>), which was interpreted as a cryovolcanic dome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sotin2005_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sotin2005-111">[111]</a></sup> No similar features have been identified as of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeCorre_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeCorre-112">[112]</a></sup> In December 2008, astronomers announced the discovery of two transient but unusually long-lived "bright spots" in Titan's atmosphere, which appear too persistent to be explained by mere weather patterns, suggesting they were the result of extended cryovolcanic episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longstaff_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-longstaff-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2009, structures resembling lava flows were announced in a region of Titan called Hotei Arcus, which appears to fluctuate in brightness over several months. Though many phenomena were suggested to explain this fluctuation, the lava flows were found to rise 200 meters (660 ft) above Titan's surface, consistent with it having been erupted from beneath the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>A mountain range measuring 150 kilometers (93 mi) long, 30 kilometers (19 mi) wide and 1.5 kilometers (0.93 mi) high was also discovered by <i>Cassini</i> in 2006. This range lies in the southern hemisphere and is thought to be composed of icy material and covered in methane snow. The movement of tectonic plates, perhaps influenced by a nearby impact basin, could have opened a gap through which the mountain's material upwelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Prior to <i>Cassini</i>, scientists assumed that most of the topography on Titan would be impact structures, yet these findings reveal that similar to Earth, the mountains were formed through geological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In December 2010, the <i>Cassini</i> mission team announced the most compelling possible cryovolcano yet found. Named <a href="/wiki/Sotra_Patera" title="Sotra Patera">Sotra Patera</a>, it is one in a chain of at least three mountains, each between 1000 and 1500 m in height, several of which are topped by large craters. The ground around their bases appears to be overlaid by frozen lava flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of Titan's highest peaks occur near its equator in so-called "ridge belts". They are believed to be analogous to Earth's <a href="/wiki/Fold_mountains" title="Fold mountains">fold mountains</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rockies" title="Rockies">Rockies</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, formed by the collision and buckling of tectonic plates, or to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subduction_zones" title="Subduction zones">subduction zones</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>, where upwelling lava (or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cryolava&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cryolava (page does not exist)">cryolava</a>) from a melting descending plate rises to the surface. One possible mechanism for their formation is tidal forces from Saturn. Because Titan's icy mantle is less viscous than Earth's magma mantle, and because its icy bedrock is softer than Earth's granite bedrock, mountains are unlikely to reach heights as great as those on Earth. In 2016, the Cassini team announced what they believe to be the tallest mountain on Titan. Located in the Mithrim Montes range, it is 3,337 m tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sotra_Facula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1431" data-file-width="2305" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sotra_Facula.jpg/220px-Sotra_Facula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sotra_Facula.jpg/330px-Sotra_Facula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sotra_Facula.jpg/440px-Sotra_Facula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sotra_Facula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens#Instruments" title="Cassini–Huygens">VIMS</a> image of the possible cryovolcano <a href="/wiki/Sotra_Patera" title="Sotra Patera">Sotra Patera</a>, combined with a 3D map based on radar data, showing 1000-meter-high peaks and a 1500-meter-deep crater.</div></div></div>
<p>If volcanism on Titan really exists, the hypothesis is that it is driven by energy released from the decay of radioactive elements within the mantle, as it is on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longstaff_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-longstaff-23">[23]</a></sup> Magma on Earth is made of liquid rock, which is less dense than the solid rocky crust through which it erupts. Because ice is less dense than water, Titan's watery magma would be denser than its solid icy crust. This means that cryovolcanism on Titan would require a large amount of additional energy to operate, possibly via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">tidal flexing</a> from nearby Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longstaff_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-longstaff-23">[23]</a></sup>  The low-pressure ice, overlaying a liquid layer of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfate" title="Ammonium sulfate">ammonium sulfate</a>, ascends buoyantly, and the unstable system can produce dramatic plume events. Titan is resurfaced through the process by grain-sized ice and ammonium sulfate ash, which helps produce a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eolian_processes" title="Eolian processes">wind-shaped</a> landscape and sand dune features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008 Jeffrey Moore (planetary geologist of <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a>) proposed an alternate view of Titan's geology. Noting that no volcanic features had been unambiguously identified on Titan so far, he asserted that Titan is a geologically dead world, whose surface is shaped only by impact cratering, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluvial" title="Fluvial">fluvial</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eolian_processes" title="Eolian processes">eolian</a> erosion, <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">mass wasting</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exogenic" title="Exogenic">exogenic</a> processes. According to this hypothesis, methane is not emitted by volcanoes but slowly diffuses out of Titan's cold and stiff interior. Ganesa Macula may be an eroded impact crater with a dark dune in the center. The mountainous ridges observed in some regions can be explained as heavily degraded <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">scarps</a> of large multi-ring impact structures or as a result of the global contraction due to the slow cooling of the interior. Even in this case, Titan may still have an internal ocean made of the eutectic water–ammonia mixture with a temperature of 176 K (−97 °C), which is low enough to be explained by the decay of radioactive elements in the core. The bright Xanadu terrain may be a degraded heavily cratered terrain similar to that observed on the surface of Callisto. Indeed, were it not for its lack of an atmosphere, Callisto could serve as a model for Titan's geology in this scenario. Jeffrey Moore even called Titan <i>Callisto with weather</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2008_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2008-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2008_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2008-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the more prominent mountains and hills have been given official names by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a>, "By convention, mountains on Titan are named for mountains from <a href="/wiki/Middle-earth" title="Middle-earth">Middle-earth</a>, the fictional setting in fantasy novels by <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>." Colles (collections of hills) are named for characters from the same Tolkien works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrr_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-jrr-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_equatorial_terrain">Dark equatorial terrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dark equatorial terrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan_dunes_crop.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="499" data-file-width="340" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Titan_dunes_crop.png/170px-Titan_dunes_crop.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Titan_dunes_crop.png/255px-Titan_dunes_crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Titan_dunes_crop.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan_dunes_crop.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sand dunes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namib_Desert" title="Namib Desert">Namib Desert</a> on Earth (top), compared with dunes in Belet on Titan</div></div></div>
<p>In the first images of Titan's surface taken by Earth-based telescopes in the early 2000s, large regions of dark terrain were revealed straddling Titan's equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roe_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roe-121">[121]</a></sup> Prior to the arrival of <i>Cassini</i>, these regions were thought to be seas of liquid hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Radar images captured by the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft have instead revealed some of these regions to be extensive plains covered in longitudinal <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a>, up to 330 ft (100 m) high<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saharan_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saharan-123">[123]</a></sup> about a kilometer wide, and tens to hundreds of kilometers long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Dunes of this type are always aligned with average wind direction. In the case of Titan, steady <a href="/wiki/Zonal_and_meridional" title="Zonal and meridional">zonal</a> (eastward) winds combine with variable tidal winds (approximately 0.5 meters per second).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorenz2006_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz2006-125">[125]</a></sup> The tidal winds are the result of <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> from Saturn on Titan's atmosphere, which are 400 times stronger than the tidal forces of the Moon on Earth and tend to drive wind toward the equator. This wind pattern, it was theorized, causes granular material on the surface to gradually build up in long parallel dunes aligned west-to-east. The dunes break up around mountains, where the wind direction shifts.
</p><p>The longitudinal (or linear) dunes were initially presumed to be formed by moderately variable winds that either follow one mean direction or alternate between two different directions. Subsequent observations indicate that the dunes point to the east although climate simulations indicate Titan's surface winds blow toward the west. At less than 1 meter per second, they are not powerful enough to lift and transport surface material. Recent computer simulations indicate that the dunes may be the result of rare storm winds that happen only every fifteen years when Titan is in <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> These storms produce strong downdrafts, flowing eastward at up to 10 meters per second when they reach the surface.
</p><p>The "sand" on Titan is likely not made up of small grains of silicates like the sand on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> but rather might have formed when liquid methane rained and eroded the water-ice bedrock, possibly in the form of flash floods. Alternatively, the sand could also have come from organic solids called <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>, produced by photochemical reactions in Titan's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saharan_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saharan-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorenz2006_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz2006-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Studies of dunes' composition in May 2008 revealed that they possessed less water than the rest of Titan, and are thus most likely derived from organic <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> like hydrocarbon polymers clumping together after raining onto the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Calculations indicate the sand on Titan has a density of one-third that of terrestrial sand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The low density combined with the dryness of Titan's atmosphere might cause the grains to clump together because of static electricity buildup. The "stickiness" might make it difficult for the generally mild breeze close to Titan's surface to move the dunes although more powerful winds from seasonal storms could still blow them eastward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around equinox, strong downburst winds can lift micron-sized solid organic particles up from the dunes to create Titanian dust storms, observed as intense and short-lived brightenings in the infrared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez2018_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez2018-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9984" data-file-width="13008" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg/400px-PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg/600px-PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg/800px-PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22484-SaturnMoon-Titan-3DustStorms-20180924.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Titan - three dust storms detected in 2009-2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180924_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180924-133">[133]</a></sup></center></div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_exploration">Observation and exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Observation and exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="485" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg/170px-Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg/255px-Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg/340px-Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan%27s_thick_haze_layer-picture_from_voyager1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> view of haze on Titan's limb (1980)</div></div></div>
<p>Titan is never visible to the naked eye, but can be observed through small telescopes or strong binoculars. Amateur observation is difficult because of the proximity of Titan to Saturn's brilliant globe and ring system; an occulting bar, covering part of the eyepiece and used to block the bright planet, greatly improves viewing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Titan has a maximum <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of +8.2,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arval_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-arval-8">[8]</a></sup> and mean opposition magnitude 8.4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-sat_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-sat-135">[135]</a></sup> This compares to +4.6<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-sat_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-sat-135">[135]</a></sup> for the similarly sized Ganymede, in the Jovian system.
</p><p>Observations of Titan prior to the space age were limited. In 1907 Spanish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Josep_Comas_i_Sol%C3%A0" title="Josep Comas i Solà">Josep Comas i Solà</a> observed <a href="/wiki/Limb_darkening" title="Limb darkening">limb darkening</a> of Titan, the first evidence that the body has an atmosphere. In 1944 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerard_P._Kuiper" title="Gerard P. Kuiper">Gerard P. Kuiper</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopic technique</a> to detect an atmosphere of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="720" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg/220px-NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg/330px-NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg/440px-NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Cassini-Saturn-TitanFlybyTests-20140617.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_(spacecraft)" title="Cassini (spacecraft)">Cassini</a>'s Titan flyby radio signal studies (artist's concept)</div></div></div>
<p>The first probe to visit the Saturnian system was <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i> in 1979, which revealed that Titan was probably too cold to support life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> It took images of Titan, including Titan and Saturn together in mid to late 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The quality was soon surpassed by the two <i>Voyagers</i>.
</p><p>Titan was examined by both <i>Voyager 1</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">2</a></i> in 1980 and 1981, respectively. <i>Voyager 1</i>'s trajectory was designed to provide an optimized Titan flyby, during which the spacecraft was able to determine the density, composition, and temperature of the atmosphere, and obtain a precise measurement of Titan's mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell2015_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell2015-139">[139]</a></sup> Atmospheric haze prevented direct imaging of the surface, though in 2004 intensive digital processing of images taken through <i>Voyager 1</i>'s orange filter did reveal hints of the light and dark features now known as <a href="/wiki/Xanadu_(Titan)" title="Xanadu (Titan)">Xanadu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shangri-la_(Titan)" title="Shangri-la (Titan)">Shangri-la</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> which had been observed in the infrared by the Hubble Space Telescope. <i>Voyager 2</i>, which would have been diverted to perform the Titan flyby if <i>Voyager 1</i> had been unable to, did not pass near Titan and continued on to Uranus and Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell2015_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bell2015-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>94</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Cassini.E2.80.93Huygens"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cassini–Huygens"><i>Cassini–Huygens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cassini–Huygens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">Huygens (spacecraft)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1231" data-file-width="1201" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg/125px-Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg/188px-Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg/250px-Titan_and_rings_PIA14909.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Cassini</i> image of Titan in front of the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA08391_Epimetheus,_Rings_and_Titan.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="1010" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg/175px-PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg/263px-PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg/350px-PIA08391_Epimetheus%2C_Rings_and_Titan.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Cassini</i> image of Titan, behind <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">Epimetheus</a> and the rings</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>Even with the data provided by the <i>Voyagers</i>, Titan remained a body of mystery—a large satellite shrouded in an atmosphere that makes detailed observation difficult. The mystery that had surrounded Titan since the 17th-century observations of Christiaan Huygens and Giovanni Cassini was revealed by a spacecraft named in their honor.
</p><p>The <i>Cassini–Huygens</i> spacecraft reached Saturn on July 1, 2004, and began the process of mapping Titan's surface by <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>. A joint project of the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, <i>Cassini–Huygens</i> proved a very successful mission. The <i>Cassini</i> probe flew by Titan on October 26, 2004, and took the highest-resolution images ever of Titan's surface, at only 1,200 kilometers (750 mi), discerning patches of light and dark that would be invisible to the human eye.
</p><p>On July 22, 2006, <i>Cassini</i> made its first targeted, close fly-by at 950 kilometers (590 mi) from Titan; the closest flyby was at 880 kilometers (550 mi) on June 21, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Liquid has been found in abundance on the surface in the north polar region, in the form of many lakes and seas discovered by <i>Cassini</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIA08630_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-PIA08630-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Huygens_landing"><i>Huygens</i> landing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Huygens landing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_surface_color.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="316" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/150px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/225px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/300px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Huygens</i> <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> image from Titan's surface—the only image from the surface of a body farther away than Mars</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="252" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg/150px-Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg/225px-Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Same image with contrast enhanced</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p><i>Huygens</i> landed on Titan on January 14, 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> discovering that many of its surface features seem to have been formed by fluids at some point in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huygens_picture_saturn_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-huygens_picture_saturn-143">[143]</a></sup>  Titan is the most distant body from Earth to have a space probe land on its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huygens_probe" title="Huygens probe"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a> landed just off the easternmost tip of a bright region now called <a href="/wiki/Adiri_(Titan)" title="Adiri (Titan)">Adiri</a>. The probe photographed pale hills with dark "rivers" running down to a dark plain. Current understanding is that the hills (also referred to as highlands) are composed mainly of water ice. Dark organic compounds, created in the upper atmosphere by the ultraviolet radiation of the Sun, may rain from Titan's atmosphere. They are washed down the hills with the methane rain and are deposited on the plains over geological time scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seeing_Touching_Titan-ESA_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seeing_Touching_Titan-ESA-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>After landing, <i>Huygens</i> photographed a dark plain covered in small rocks and pebbles, which are composed of water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seeing_Touching_Titan-ESA_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seeing_Touching_Titan-ESA-145">[145]</a></sup> The two rocks just below the middle of the image on the right are smaller than they may appear: the left-hand one is 15 centimeters across, and the one in the center is 4 centimeters across, at a distance of about 85 centimeters from <i>Huygens</i>. There is evidence of erosion at the base of the rocks, indicating possible fluvial activity. The surface is darker than originally expected, consisting of a mixture of water and hydrocarbon ice. The "soil" visible in the images is interpreted to be precipitation from the hydrocarbon haze above.
</p><p>In March 2007, NASA, ESA, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COSPAR" title="COSPAR">COSPAR</a> decided to name the <i>Huygens</i> landing site the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubert_Curien" title="Hubert Curien">Hubert Curien</a> Memorial Station</i> in memory of the former president of the ESA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_or_conceptual_missions">Proposed or conceptual missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Proposed or conceptual missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="472" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg/170px-TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg/255px-TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg/340px-TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TSSM-TandEM-Montgolfiere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The balloon proposed for the Titan Saturn System Mission (artistic rendition)</div></div></div>
<p>There have been several conceptual missions proposed in recent years for returning a robotic <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> to Titan.  Initial conceptual work has been completed for such missions by NASA, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a>.  At present, none of these proposals have become funded missions.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">Titan Saturn System Mission</a> (TSSM) was a joint NASA/<a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> proposal for exploration of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> It envisions a hot-air balloon floating in Titan's atmosphere for six months. It was competing against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_Jupiter_System_Mission" title="Europa Jupiter System Mission">Europa Jupiter System Mission</a> (EJSM) proposal for funding. In February 2009 it was announced that ESA/NASA had given the EJSM mission priority ahead of the TSSM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proposed <a href="/wiki/Titan_Mare_Explorer" title="Titan Mare Explorer">Titan Mare Explorer</a> (TiME) was a low-cost lander that would splash down in a lake in Titan's northern hemisphere and float on the surface of the lake for three to six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talk_2010_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-talk_2010-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-151">[151]</a></sup>  It was selected for a Phase-A design study in 2011 as a candidate mission for the 12th NASA <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a> opportunity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> but was not selected for flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another mission to Titan proposed in early 2012 by Jason Barnes, a scientist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Idaho" title="University of Idaho">University of Idaho</a>, is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerial_Vehicle_for_In-situ_and_Airborne_Titan_Reconnaissance" title="Aerial Vehicle for In-situ and Airborne Titan Reconnaissance">Aerial Vehicle for In-situ and Airborne Titan Reconnaissance</a> (AVIATR): an unmanned plane (or <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">drone</a>) that would fly through Titan's atmosphere and take <a href="/wiki/High-definition_video" title="High-definition video">high-definition</a> images of the surface of Titan. NASA did not approve the requested $715 million, and the future of the project is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>A conceptual design for another lake lander was proposed in late 2012 by the Spanish-based private engineering firm <a href="/wiki/SENER" title="SENER">SENER</a> and the Centro de Astrobiología in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>. The concept probe is called <a href="/wiki/Titan_Lake_In-situ_Sampling_Propelled_Explorer" title="Titan Lake In-situ Sampling Propelled Explorer">Titan Lake In-situ Sampling Propelled Explorer</a> (TALISE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TALISE_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-TALISE-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The major difference compared to the TiME probe would be that TALISE is envisioned with its own propulsion system and would therefore not be limited to simply drifting on the lake when it splashes down.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a> contestant for its mission #13 is <a href="/wiki/Journey_to_Enceladus_and_Titan" title="Journey to Enceladus and Titan">Journey to Enceladus and Titan</a> (JET), an <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> Saturn orbiter that would assess the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability potential</a> of <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a> and Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sotin2011_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sotin2011-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCPM-2013_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-LCPM-2013-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JET_204_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-JET_204-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Institute_for_Advanced_Concepts" title="NASA Institute for Advanced Concepts">NASA Innovative Advanced Concepts</a> program (NIAC) awarded a Phase II grant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> to a design study of a submarine to explore the seas of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prebiotic_conditions_and_life">Prebiotic conditions and life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prebiotic conditions and life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Titan" title="Life on Titan">Life on Titan</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">Planetary habitability</a></div>
<p>Titan is thought to be a <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">prebiotic environment</a> rich in complex organic chemistry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysOrg-20130403_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhysOrg-20130403-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conversation_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conversation-165">[165]</a></sup> with a possible subsurface liquid ocean serving as a <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">biotic environment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grasset2000_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grasset2000-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortes2000_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fortes2000-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life?_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-life?-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Cassini–Huygens</i> mission was not equipped to provide evidence for <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> or complex <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>; it showed an environment on Titan that is similar, in some ways, to ones theorized for the primordial Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raulin2005_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raulin2005-169">[169]</a></sup> Scientists surmise that the atmosphere of early Earth was similar in composition to the current atmosphere on Titan, with the important exception of a lack of water vapor on Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conversation_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conversation-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_complex_molecules">Formation of complex molecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Formation of complex molecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a> and several following experiments have shown that with an atmosphere similar to that of Titan and the addition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a>, complex molecules and polymer substances like <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a> can be generated. The reaction starts with <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociation</a> of nitrogen and methane, forming hydrogen cyanide and acetylene. Further reactions have been studied extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raulin2002_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raulin2002-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been reported that when energy was applied to a combination of gases like those in Titan's atmosphere, five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleotide_bases" title="Nucleotide bases">nucleotide bases</a>, the building blocks of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, were among the many compounds produced. In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>, the building blocks of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> were found. It was the first time nucleotide bases and amino acids had been found in such an experiment without liquid water being present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 3, 2013, NASA reported that complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_chemicals" title="Organic chemicals">organic chemicals</a> could arise on Titan based on studies simulating the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)#Atmosphere" title="Titan (moon)">atmosphere</a> of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysOrg-20130403_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-PhysOrg-20130403-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 6, 2013, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Andaluc%C3%ADa" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Andalucía">IAA-CSIC</a> reported the detection of <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAH) in the upper atmosphere of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAA-20130606_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAA-20130606-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 26, 2017, Cassini scientists positively identified the presence of carbon chain anions in Titan's upper atmosphere which appeared to be involved in the production of large complex organics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
These highly reactive molecules were previously known to contribute to building complex organics in the Interstellar Medium, therefore highlighting a possibly universal stepping stone to producing complex organic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 28, 2017, scientists reported that <a href="/wiki/Acrylonitrile" title="Acrylonitrile">acrylonitrile</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinyl_cyanide" title="Vinyl cyanide">vinyl cyanide</a>, (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>CN), possibly essential for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> by being related to <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry)" title="Vesicle (biology and chemistry)">vesicle structure</a> formation, had been found on Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20170728_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20170728-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA-20170728_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-20170728-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170808_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170808-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2018, researchers reported low-temperature chemical pathways from simple <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> to complex <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon</a> (PAH) chemicals. Such chemical pathways may help explain the presence of PAHs in the low-temperature atmosphere of Titan, and may be significant pathways, in terms of the <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">PAH world hypothesis</a>, in producing presursors to biochemcals related to life as we know it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DG-20181008_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-DG-20181008-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA-20181008_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA-20181008-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_subsurface_habitats">Possible subsurface habitats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Possible subsurface habitats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Laboratory simulations have led to the suggestion that enough organic material exists on Titan to start a chemical evolution analogous to what is thought to have started life on Earth. The analogy assumes the presence of liquid water for longer periods than is currently observable; several theories suggest that liquid water from an impact could be preserved under a frozen isolation layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> It has also been theorized that liquid-ammonia oceans could exist deep below the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grasset2000_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grasset2000-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Another model suggests an ammonia–water solution as much as 200 kilometers (120 mi) deep beneath a water-ice crust with conditions that, although extreme by terrestrial standards, are such that life could survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortes2000_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fortes2000-167">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">Heat transfer</a> between the interior and upper layers would be critical in sustaining any subsurface oceanic life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grasset2000_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grasset2000-166">[166]</a></sup> Detection of microbial life on Titan would depend on its biogenic effects, with the atmospheric methane and nitrogen examined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortes2000_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fortes2000-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_and_life_at_the_surface">Methane and life at the surface</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Methane and life at the surface">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry" title="Hypothetical types of biochemistry">Hypothetical types of biochemistry</a></div>
<p>It has been suggested that life could exist in the lakes of liquid methane on Titan, just as organisms on Earth live in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mckay_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-mckay-182">[182]</a></sup> Such organisms would inhale H<sub>2</sub> in place of O<sub>2</sub>, metabolize it with <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, and exhale methane instead of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life?_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-life?-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mckay_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-mckay-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>All living things on Earth (including methanogens) use liquid water as a solvent; it is speculated that life on Titan might instead use a liquid hydrocarbon, such as methane or ethane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methanesolvent_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-methanesolvent-183">[183]</a></sup> Water is a stronger solvent than methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methlife_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-methlife-184">[184]</a></sup> Water is also more chemically reactive, and can break down large organic molecules through <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methanesolvent_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-methanesolvent-183">[183]</a></sup> A life-form whose solvent was a hydrocarbon would not face the risk of its biomolecules being destroyed in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methanesolvent_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-methanesolvent-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrobiologist" title="Astrobiologist">astrobiologist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christopher_McKay_(planetary_scientist)" title="Christopher McKay (planetary scientist)">Chris McKay</a> argued that if methanogenic life did exist on the surface of Titan, it would likely have a measurable effect on the mixing ratio in the Titan troposphere: levels of hydrogen and acetylene would be measurably lower than otherwise expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mckay_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-mckay-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, Darrell Strobel, from <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a>, identified a greater abundance of molecular hydrogen in the upper atmospheric layers of Titan compared to the lower layers, arguing for a downward flow at a rate of roughly 10<sup>28</sup> molecules per second and disappearance of hydrogen near Titan's surface; as Strobel noted, his findings were in line with the effects McKay had predicted if <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanogenic" title="Methanogenic">methanogenic</a> life-forms were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mckay_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-mckay-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methlife_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-methlife-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The same year, another study showed low levels of acetylene on Titan's surface, which were interpreted by McKay as consistent with the hypothesis of organisms consuming hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methlife_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-methlife-184">[184]</a></sup> Although restating the biological hypothesis, he cautioned that other explanations for the hydrogen and acetylene findings are more likely: the possibilities of yet unidentified physical or chemical processes (e.g. a surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> accepting hydrocarbons or hydrogen), or flaws in the current models of material flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life?_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-life?-168">[168]</a></sup> Composition data and transport models need to be substantiated, etc. Even so, despite saying that a non-biological catalytic explanation would be less startling than a biological one, McKay noted that the discovery of a catalyst effective at 95 K (−180 °C) would still be significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life?_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-life?-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>As NASA notes in its news article on the June 2010 findings: "To date, methane-based life forms are only hypothetical. Scientists have not yet detected this form of life anywhere."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methlife_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-methlife-184">[184]</a></sup> As the NASA statement also says: "some scientists believe these chemical signatures bolster the argument for a primitive, exotic form of life or precursor to life on Titan's surface."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methlife_184-4"><a href="#cite_note-methlife-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2015, a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> capable of functioning in liquid <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in Titan conditions was modeled. Composed of small molecules containing carbon, hydrogen, and nitrogen, it would have the same stability and flexibility as cell membranes on Earth, which are composed of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, compounds of carbon, hydrogen, oxygen, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>. This hypothetical cell membrane was termed an "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azotosome" title="Azotosome">azotosome</a>", a combination of "azote", French for nitrogen, and "<a href="/wiki/Liposome" title="Liposome">liposome</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-azotosomemodel_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-azotosomemodel-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obstacles">Obstacles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Obstacles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite these biological possibilities, there are formidable obstacles to life on Titan, and any analogy to Earth is inexact. At a vast distance from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, Titan is frigid, and its atmosphere lacks CO<sub>2</sub>. At Titan's surface, water exists only in solid form. Because of these difficulties, scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Lunine" title="Jonathan Lunine">Jonathan Lunine</a> have viewed Titan less as a likely habitat for life, than as an experiment for examining theories on the conditions that prevailed prior to the appearance of life on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Although life itself may not exist, the prebiotic conditions on Titan and the associated organic chemistry remain of great interest in understanding the early history of the terrestrial biosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raulin2005_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raulin2005-169">[169]</a></sup> Using Titan as a prebiotic experiment involves not only observation through spacecraft, but laboratory experiments, and chemical and photochemical modeling on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raulin2002_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raulin2002-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panspermia_hypothesis">Panspermia hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Panspermia hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is hypothesized that large asteroid and cometary impacts on Earth's surface may have caused fragments of microbe-laden rock to escape Earth's gravity, suggesting the possibility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transpermia" title="Transpermia">transpermia</a>. Calculations indicate that these would encounter many of the bodies in the Solar System, including Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> On the other hand, Jonathan Lunine has argued that any living things in Titan's cryogenic hydrocarbon lakes would need to be so different chemically from Earth life that it would not be possible for one to be the ancestor of the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_conditions">Future conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titan_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Future conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conditions on Titan could become far more <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a> in the far future. Five billion years from now, as the Sun becomes a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>, its surface temperature could rise enough for Titan to support liquid water on its surface, making it habitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> As the Sun's ultraviolet output decreases, the haze in Titan's upper atmosphere will be depleted, lessening the anti-greenhouse effect on the surface and enabling the greenhouse created by atmospheric methane to play a far greater role. These conditions together could create a habitable environment, and could persist for several hundred million years. This is proposed to have been sufficient time for simple life to spawn on Earth, though the presence of ammonia on Titan would cause chemical reactions to proceed more slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>

<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Titanian" is the adjectival form of both Titan and Uranus's moon <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>. The latter may be pronounced with an <i><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ah</i></a></i> vowel (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>), but the form for Titan is only pronounced with an <i><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ay</i></a></i> vowel: <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>. The less common "Titanean" <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> refers only to Titan.</span>
</li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 10,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Earth+could+seed+Titan+with+life&amp;rft.date=2006-03-18&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fnews.bbc.co.uk%2F2%2Fhi%2Fscience%2Fnature%2F4819370.stm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATitan+%28moon%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
